for the Financial Year 2023-2024

Dr. Maj Gen Md Abu Sayed Siddique (Retd)

Disclaimer

This  work  is  a  product  of  the  consultant  engaged  by  BPPA,  IMED,  Ministry  of  Planning.  The
findings,  interpretations,  and  conclusions  expressed  in  this  work  do  not  necessarily  reflect  the
views  of  the  government.  BPPA  does  not  guarantee  the  accuracy  of  the  data  included  in  this
publication and accepts no responsibility for any consequence of their use. The purposes of the
dissemination  of  this  report  are  to  support  procuring  entities  and  help  them  to  take  remedial
actions  of  the  observations/recommendations  of  the  report.  It  also  facilitates  them  to  improve
procurement transactions. BPPA has all rights reserved of the document.

taking  the  initiative  to  conduct  Procurement  Post  Review  in  accordance  with  the  provisions  of
PPA 2006 and PPR 2008, and for providing me with the opportunity to work as an independent
consultant  to  conduct  a  procurement  post-review  of  Directorate  General  of  Health  Services
(DGHS) procurement contracts for the financial year 2023-24.

other Officials of DGHS for providing me with the necessary documents and information.

Value for Money

Executive Summary

Bangladesh has been implementing public procurement reform since 2002, with an emphasis on
institutional  and  legal  frameworks,  as  well  as  capacity-building  initiatives.  Bangladesh  Public
Procurement  Authority  (BPPA)  encourages  optimal  resource  use,  transparency,  accountability,
efficiency, and fair competition in public procurement. The BPPA uses resources and independent
experts to analyze procurement records, including a consultant who will review post-procurement
contracts for the Directorate General of Health Services (DGHS) in fiscal year 2023–24.

The  selection  of  projects/operational  plans  and  contracts  for  review  has  been  meticulous,
considering  various  procurement
types,  methods,  categories,  contract  values,  and
projects/operational plans funded by the government and development partners. Initially, fourteen
projects  and  same  number  of  Operational  Plan  (OP)  were  considered,  but  five  project/OP  were
selected using a project/OP evaluation matrix, resulting in thirty contracts from there for review.

DGHS  prepared  the  APP  but  it’s  not  available  on  their  website.  Procurement  planning  was
generally  meticulous, with packages and lots prepared following DPP/OP and APP rules except
few  cases.  Advertisements  were  done  in  newspapers  and  websites,  except  for  DPM  and  SSS.
Committees were formed according to rules, except for the formation of OCEC in one OP. Tender
evaluations were detailed. Contract documents were prepared properly except DPM. There were
additional  contacts  ranging  from  10%  to  42.25%  in  OP  2  and  3.    Some  documents  were  not
maintained properly.

In  financial  year  2023-24,  DGHS  invited  90.83%  of  tenders  against  their  APP  and  84.87%
contracts were signed. Out of 30 selected contracts, 26 are in the goods category, accounting for
86.66% of the total tender; and 4 are in the services category, accounting for 13.34% of the total
tender. 83.33% of the tender under review followed OTM, with 6.67% using QCBS and 3.33%
each  using  DPM,  RFQ,  and  SSS  methods.  100%  of  the  tenders  followed  National  Competitive
Tendering. 60% of the selected tenders used e-GP mode, while 40% were done manually, but in
contrast their contract value was 23.97% and 76.03% respectively. All contracts followed the PPR
2008 and e-GP guidelines. The total contracted price was 8.13% lower than the OCE price.

The  number  and  percentage  of  single  tender  and  single  responsive  tenders  among  the  selected
contracts are high with 43.33% of total contracts and 82.88% total contracted value shared by 5

10

tenderers. DGHS needs to improve contract implementation, as the average time overrun is 433
days.  Delays  occurred  for  tendering  and  contracting  at  the  fag  end  of  the  financial  year  in  the
month  of  June  and  also  for  contracting  additional  delivery.  Tenders  were  given  with  adequate
preparation time and all  NoA were issued timely. 73.33% tenders were evaluated within the time
line, and 80% tenders followed the DoFP.

Field visits and physical inspection results revealed that some goods were procured without the
requirement. Equipment has been delivered but not installed, and the condition deteriorated in the
storehouse. These findings highlight the need to prioritize efficient, effective, and economical use
of procured goods to achieve value for money.

The  SWOT  analysis  reveals  that  tender  evaluation,  and  use  of    consultants,  to  augment
procurement  capability  have  been  good  but  have  weaknesses  in  limited  supplier  options,  less
competition, lacking needs assessment, inadequate maintenance of the equipment, weak contract
management, and resource constraints. To improve the procurement system, they should focus on
market  research,  expand  supplier  bases,  and  provide  staff  training  to  enhance  the  procurement
capacity. Addressing issues like supplier irrational justifications and mitigating reputational risk
for late delivery of the medicine are also necessary.

To  ensure  transparency  and  accountability  in  the  procurement  system,  it  is  recommended  to
conduct  a  thorough  needs  assessment  to  ensure  that  goods  are  used  for  their  intended  purpose.
Efforts should be made to maximize the value of each purchase or sum of money spent. Procured
goods  should  be  delivered  within  the  specific  delivery  time.  Estimating  the  budget  with  the
procurement  timeframe  and  aligning  the  budget  within  the  timeline  is  important  for  efficient
financial  and  budget  management.  Budget  planning  and  timeline  should  be  based  on  realistic
assumptions so that tenders, contracts, and delivery of goods are well planned within the financial
year. Practice of payment to the supplier by the PE before the delivery of goods should be avoided
at all cost.

DPM approved in the APP should not be split into small packages with the obligations of seeking
the approval of higher authority. The goods procured through DPM should also be taken from the
appropriate supplier, and trading through DPM should be avoided. Assessing suppliers' capacity is
crucial  to  avoid  delays  in  delivery  and  contract  implementation.  Competitive  tendering  and
adhering  to  the  different  rules  of  the  PPR  2008  are  essential  for  maintaining  transparency  and
accountability  in  the  procurement.  Last  but  not  least,  staff  training  can  also  enhance  the
procurement capacity.

11

Chapter One

Introduction and Methodology

1.1  Introduction

As part of its efforts to increase overall sectoral administration, the  Bangladesh government has
launched a comprehensive effort to improve public procurement performance. A permanent entity
called  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA),  based  in  the  Implementation
Monitoring and Evaluation Division (IMED) of the Ministry of Planning, was established as an
institutional reform with the goal of promoting efficient, effective, and cost-effective resource use
in public procurement. Through the legislative frameworks of the Public Procurement Act 2006
(PPA-2006) and the Public Procurement R ules 2008 (PPR 2008), the government has made great
strides in laying the foundation for its public procurement system.

1.2  Background and Context

The PPA 2006 and the PPR 2008 were approved by the Government of the People's Republic of
Bangladesh  in  order  to  encourage  transparency,  accountability,  efficiency,  and  fair  competition
when  purchasing  goods,  and  services  using  public  funds.    According  to  Section  24  of  the  PPA
2006  and  Rules  45  and  46  of  the  PPR  2008,  the  BPPA  is  in  charge  of  keeping  an  eye  on  and
assessing  the  PPA  and  PPR  implementation  in  procurement  management  in  agencies  that  use
public funds.

As  per  the  regulations,  if  the  total  value  of  the  procurements  that  a  Procuring  Entity  (PE)
completes  in  a  financial  year  is  more  than  Tk.  10.00  crore,  the  BPPA  will  examine  the
procurement  records  of  the  PE  using  its  own  resources  and  outside  experts.  The  independent
consultants  will  verify  adherence  to  the  Act's  regulations  and  the  procurement  process's  rules,
while the BPPA will designate which PE will be assessed.

To examine the sample procurement contracts carried out by the government's chosen agencies,
BPPA hired Procurement Post Review Consultants in light of the aforementioned.  The consultant
has  been  assigned  the  responsibility  of  examining  the  Directorate  General  of  Health  Services
(DGHS) procurement post contracts.

1.3  Objectives of the Assignment

Through the review of records and other relevant documents pertaining to the sample contracts,
the  assignment's  overall  objective  is  to  ensure  the  efficient,  effective,  and  economical  use  of
resources in public procurement by verifying compliance with the provisions of PPA, 2006, and
PPR, 2008, as well as the guidelines and regulations of development partners in the procurement
process.  This  will  ensure  value  for  money  in  public  procurement  transactions.  Here  are  some
specific objectives:

12

a.  Assess  if  the  contract  procedures  and  procurement  procedures  comply  with  the  PPA,

2006, and PPR, 2008.

b.  Examine  if  the  official  cost  estimate  was  prepared  in  accordance  with  PPR,  2008,  and
whether  the  authorizing  authority  made  any  modifications  and  opened  it  in  accordance
with the regulations.

c.  Examine  if  tender  documents  are  prepared  in  accordance  with  the  Acts  and  Rules  and

whether STDs are observed.

d.  Evaluate the  degree to  which  the  PE's  mandated Value-for-Money  (VfM)  has  been  met

during the procurement process.

e.  Examine  and  evaluate  the  relevant  Procuring  Entities'  ability  to  handle  procurement  in
accordance  with  rules  and  necessary  standards,  from  document  creation  to  contract
implementation and document maintenance.

f.  Examine and determine whether the purchases are being used for the intended purpose.
g.  Determine  how  to  strengthen  the  procurement  contracts  in  light  of  the  shortcomings

found in the earlier evaluation.

h.  Any further matters that might seem relevant.

1.4  Scopes of the Assignment

The procurement process of Procuring Entity shall be reviewed if the total procurement performed
in  a  financial  year  worth  at  least  taka  10.00  crore  or  more  as  per  Rule  46(7)  of  PPR,  2008  on
sample  basis  of  the  procurement  contracts  completed/awarded  in  the  financial  year  2023-2024.
Total six (6) procurement contracts from each of five (5) Procuring Entities of the Government of
Bangladesh will be reviewed. The scopes of the assignments are as under:

a.  Verify  the  procurement  and  contract  proceeding  executed  by  the  procuring  entity  and

identify compliance and non-compliance with the procurement Act and Rules.

b.  Verify whether PE has used Standard Tender  Documents (STDs) and Standard Request
for Proposals (SRfPs) in its Procurement content & put necessary information in Tender
Data Sheet (TDS), Proposal data Sheet (PDS) and other formats correctly.

c.  Verify,  to  the  extent  possible,  whether  the  Goods,  Works  and  Services  contracted  were
supplied/performed  and/or  completed  according  to  the  required  specifications  and
technical standards.

d.  Identify inappropriate practices or questionable decisions and/or actions.
e.  Verify whether documentation and record keeping systems that are in place are adequate

for ensuring post review requirements.

f.  Verify  whether  goods  and  works  exist  at  intended  locations  and  are  being  used  for  the

purposes they were acquired.

g.  Determine remedies to correct the identified deficiencies.

For each contract package following has been reviewed:

a.  Procurement plan, budget, official cost estimate.
b.  Procurement  type,  category,  method,  objective,  financing  arrangement  (in  case  of  DP’s

supported project).

c.  Procurement opportunities.
d.  Tender/proposal submissions.
e.  Formation of TOC/POC and TEC/PEC (no of members, external members).

13

f.  Tenders/Proposals  Evaluation  (opening,  days  between  opening  and  evaluation,
responsiveness  (responsive  and  non-responsive),  recommendations  of  the  evaluation
committee).
g.  Post qualification.
h.  Approval of tenders/proposals (actual days between opening and evaluation and between
evaluation  and  approval,  approval  authority  as  delegation  of  financial  power,  actual
approval authority, actual days for issuance of NOA/contract signing/LoI.

i.  Contract award (procurement processing lag and lead time).
j.  Completion  of  contract  (time  specified  for  supply/execution/perform/  Pre-shipment

Inspection/ Post Landing Inspection).

k.  Complaints and appeal (reason of complain, resolution of complaints, decision of review

panel).

l.  Events of realising liquidated damage (if any).
m.  Contract  amendment  (no  of  amendment,  contract  time  extension,  variation/extra  work/

repeat/additional delivery orders / price adjustment, etc.).

n.  Contract disputes unresolved.
o.  Action on corruption, fraudulence, collusive and coercive if identified.
p.  Procurement management capacity (no of staff trained in procurement) etc.
q.  Asset/ Inventory Management.
r.  Audit objection and status.

Methodology

1.5 Evaluation Approach

a. Evaluation Principles

The evaluation has been   carried out by obtaining answers to pertinent questions and reviewing
documents adhering to the acts and rules outlined in the PPA 2006 and PPR 2008.

b. Evaluation Questions

Throughout the review, answers to the following questions have been sought:

i.    How effective is procurement planning?

ii.   What is the situation of procurement competition?

iii.  What is the current level of performance efficiency?

iv.   How is the efficiency of procurement implementation?

v.    What is the status of tenders with complaints?

vi.   What is the state of Accessibility in the procurement process?

vii.  What is the state of Market/Suppliers Diversification?

viii.  What is the state of corruption (if any)?

 ix.   What is the state of Transparency and Accountability in the procurement process?

14

1.7 Project and Contract Sampling

The considerations sample selection for reviewing projects and contracts are as under:

a.  Procurement  of  goods,  works  and  related  services  contracts,  including  contracts  involving
supply and installation.

b. Procurement of consulting services and physical services.

c. Any other unique procurement.

d. Samples from both national and international procurement.

e. Samples from development and revenue budgets.

f. Samples from both funded by GoB and donors and in combination, if applicable.

g.  Samples  of  very  small/simple  contracts,  small/less  complex  contracts  and  the  more
elaborate/complex contracts.

h. The value of the contracts: low value, medium value and high value;

i.  The  geographical  spread  of  the  contracts  and  select  procurement  involving  contracts  from
different regions (if applicable).

j. Samples involving both Pre-qualification and Post-qualification (goods and works).

k. Samples involving Expression of Interest, Short-listing (services)

l. Method of procurement

m. Method of selection of consultants through Expression of  Interest (short listing), Request for
Proposal (RFP) and Single Source Selection; selection criteria (with or without price as a factor).

n. Competition in Procurement.

o. Price Adjustment: with and without application.

p. Complaint and Appeal/Dispute Resolutions: tendering and contract stages.

q. Contract Modifications/variations: with and without permission.

1.8 Methods of Data Collection and Data Analysis

Data has been  collected in two phases. Following methods will be used for data collection:

a. Phase-I:

(i)  Previous post review reports.
(ii)  Meeting with the Project Directors/Line Directors of the selected projects.

18

(iii) Procurement related documents of selected contracts from project office.
(iv) Financial documentation.
(v)  Structured Questionnaire.

b. Phase-II:

(i)  Documentary  review  of  the  selected  procurement  contracts  for  Goods,  Works  and

Services in specified formats.

(ii)  Documentary  review  of  procurement  process,  done  by  the  Project  Directors/Line

Directors, DGHS at the respective area and Project office.

(iii)  Physical Inspections at the project site.
(iv)  A  Local  Workshop  was  conducted  on  21  April  25  at  DGHS  and  the  findings  were
apprised  where  ADG,  Director  (Planning  and  research),  all  LDs  and  other  concerned
officers were present. CEO,  and other officials of BPPA were also present. On 26 May
25, a meeting was held at DGHS where all PD/LD were present. The report was apprised
to all, and their feedback was taken and incorporated in the report.

1.9 Evaluation of Data

All collected data, information, feedback, opinions, and comments has been objectively analyzed
and  evaluated  using  different  Acts  and  Rules  of  PPA  2006,  PPR  2008  and  e  -GP  guidelines  to
derive appropriate findings and recommendations.

1.10 Deliverables

The following deliverables to be submitted to BPPA as per given schedule and format:

a.   Preparation and submission of Inception Report.

b.   Preparation and submission of Interim Progress Report.

c.   Preparation and Submission of Draft Final Report

d.   Submission of Final Report.

20

Quality Assurance

1.12  The  consultant  performing  the  review  process  has  maintained  the  highest  professional
standards. The consultant has adopted the following strategy to achieve quality assurance:

a.  Full dedication to the job and use of knowledge and expertise.

b.  Consultation  with  the  supervisor  and  other  Directors  of  BPPA  for  clarifications  and

suggestions regarding the review.

Ethical Considerations

1.13  Procurement and its review are sensitive issues as they involve government money. As such,
the highest ethical standard is assured by the consultants. The following approach has been taken
by the consultant to ensure ethical standards:

a.  Complete adherence to the BPPA Terms of Reference.
b.  Confidentiality of the documents and information.
c.  Confidentiality of the review report.
d.  While some unintentional errors may occur, the overall aim of the  review is to assist in

improving the procurement system of DGHS.

Limitations

1.14  Following are the few limitations observed during the review:

a.  Given  the  scope  of  the  review,  the  dispersion  of  different  locations  and  site  areas,  the
deployment  of  procured  goods  in  various  locations,  and  the  involvement  of  numerous
stakeholders, completing the review within the time frame posed a significant challenge.

b.  Many documents, particularly those for manual procurement, took a long time to locate,

which was also a limiting issue in collecting the data.

21

2.5 Appraisal of Selected Project/Operational Plan

a. Project-1: Expansion of National Institute of Neurosciences and Hospital Project (NINS)

i. Project Implementation Period: 01 January 2018 to  31 December 2025.

ii. Estimated Cost: BDT 50613.95 Lakh.

iii. Objectives of the Project:

(a) To enhance the capacity of the hospital from 500 to 1000 bedded and provide outdoor
and indoor treatment facility to the neurosurgery and neurology patient.
(b)  To  decrease  the  mortality  rate  of  the  neuro  patient  by  providing  the  specialized
treatment.
(c)  To  providing  world  standard  training  to  the  doctors,  nurses  and  paramedics  for
treating the neuro patients.
(d) To enhance research on neurosurgery and neurology treatment.
(e)  To  enhance  the  confidence  on  neurology  treatment  within  the  country  and  thereby
decreasing the treatment abroad, thereby saving the foreign currency.
(f) To increase the capacity and impart higher education like MD, MS and FCPS.

b. Operational Plan-2: Upazila Health Care (UHC)

i. Operational Plan Implementation Period: January 2017 to 30 June 2024.

ii. Estimated Cost: BDT 151216.36 Lakh.

iii.  General  Objective:  To  ensure  healthy  lives  and  promoting  well-being  for  all
increasing  accessibility,  affordability  and  utilizing  of  primary  healthcare  services  from
UzHC, other hospitals within upazila and Union Sub Centers  .

iv. Specific Objectives of the Operational Plan:
(a)  Strengthening  Upazila  Health  care  management  and  services  by  establishing  an

effective health system.

(b)  Establishing functional referral system within and beyond Upazila.
(c)  Ensuring  supplies  (drugs,  equipment,  instruments,  logistics,  etc.)  for  proper
functioning UzHC, other hospitals (10, 20 bedded hospitals and Trauma Centers) and
Union sub Centers within Upazila under DGHS.

28

(d)  Institutional Development ( Infrastructure, proper staffing and all necessary supplies)

at different levels of health facilities within Upazila to improve performance.

(e)  Effective  management  of  medical  waste  UzHC  and  other  hospitals  (10,  20  bedded
hospitals and Trauma Centers) and Union sub Centers within Upazila under DGHS.
(f)  Improving  urban  health  through  enhanced  utilization  of  existing  public  health

facilities.

(g)  Improving  access and  utilization  of  quality  public  health  care services for  the  tribal

population both hill and plain districts.

c. Operational Plan-3: Non-Communicable Disease Control (NCDC)

i. Operational Plan Implementation Period: January 2017 to June 2024.

ii. Estimated Cost: BDT 113499.00 Lakh.

iii. General Objective: To reduce mortality and morbidity of non-communicable diseases
in Bangladesh through control of risk factors and improving health service delivery.

iv. Specific Objectives of the Operational Plan:
(a)  To promote and development of effective, integrated, sustainable, and evidence-based
public policies for non-communicable disease, their risk factors and determinants.
(b)  To  develop  and  strengthen  capacity  for  surveillance  of  non-communicable  disease,
their  consequences,  their  risk  factors  and,  and  the  impact  on  public  health
interventions.

(c)  To  foster,  support,  and  promote  social  and  economic  conditions  that  address  the
determinants  of  chromonic  non  communicable  disease  and  empower  people  to
increase control over their health and to adopt healthy behaviors.

(d)  To strengthen the capacity and competencies of the health system for the integrated
early  detection  management  and  control  the  risk  factors  of  non-communicable
diseases.

d. Operational Plan-4: Communicable Disease Control (CDC)

i. Operational Plan Implementation Period: January 2017 to June 2024.

ii. Estimated Cost: BDT 118835.00 Lakh

iii. General Objective: To control/eliminate communicable diseases from Bangladesh.

iv. Specific Objectives of the Operational Plan:

(a)  Reduce malaria API to less than 1 per 1,000 population at risk in CHT districts and
eliminate indigenous malaria in phased manner in 10 other endemic districts by 2025.
(b)  To  prevent  and  control  Aedes  transmitted  viral  diseases  including  dengue,

chikungunya and zika.

(c)  To achieve filaria elimination (mf <1%) status by 2022.
(d)  To reduce prevalence of STH along 5-16 years children to less than 6% within 2023.
(e)  To achieve Kala-azar free Bangladesh by 2030.
(f)  To eliminate rabies by 2030.

29

(g)  To develop core capacity at designated points of entry in compliance with IHR 2005
to  prevent  international  spread  of  diseases  like  COVID-19,  Ebola  through  health
screening of passengers at point of entries.

(h)  To  detect,  asses,  notify,  report,  and  respond  to  public  health  emergency  of

international concern (PHEIC).

(i)  To prevent spread of communicable disease among FDMN.

e. Operational Plan -5: Alternative Medical Care (AMC)

i. Operational Plan Implementation Period: January 2017 to June 2024.

ii. Estimated Cost: BDT 32986.00 Lakh (BDT 30937.00 Lakh GoB and BDT 2049.00
Lakh RPA).

iii. General Objective: To scale up Unani, Ayurvedic and Homeopathic Medical services
throughout the  country  along  with the  allopathic  system  to  ensure  quality  and  equitable
health  services  for  all  citizen  of  Bangladesh  and  develop  Unani,  Ayurvedic  and
Homeopathic system of medical education.

iv. Specific Objectives of the Operational Plan:

(a)  To develop and expand  Unani, Ayurvedic and Homeopathic Medical services as an

effective treatment system and give them institutional shape.

(b)  To  monitor  and  control  AMC  participation  by  strengthening  and  making  functional

regulatory bodies of Unani, Ayurvedic and Homeopathic registration council.

(c)  To establish a central research council and national herbal garden for academic and

research purpose.

(d)  To develop a strict regulation to control the quality of AMC drugs in the country.
(e)  To  upgrade  the  existing    Unani,  Ayurvedic  and  Homeopathic  Medical  College  and

Hospital.

(f)  To develop capacity of AMC service providers by conducting  workshop, orientation

and training.

(g)  To approve national strategy and action plan and developed on Unani, Ayurvedic and

Homeopathic system.

(h)  To explore people’s perceptions about  Unani, Ayurvedic and Homeopathic medicine
by  providing  quality  services  and  thus  reduces  unsound  practices  in  the  name  of
AMC.

2.7      Limitations in Selection of Projects/Operational Plan and Contracts

a. Difficulties in getting the information about the operational plan. Information regarding
the operational plan and contracts was not received from some PEs.  Information on not
providing the operational plan has been attached to this report.

b. Many of the projects do not have adequate progress and the projects are mostly related
to the Works, which is done by the Public Works Department.

Chapter Three

35

Data Analysis and Findings

Procurement Management Capacity of DGHS

3.1 Brief Appraisal on DGHS

The DGHS is one of the agencies of the Ministry of Health & Family Welfare of Bangladesh. The
main functions of this agency implementation of health programs and services for the ministry of
health.  In  addition,  the  DGHS  provides  technical  assistance  to  the  ministry.  The  DGHS  was
established  as  directorate  in  1958  during  the  Pakistan  era.  In  1980,  it  has  been  upgraded  to
Directorate  General.  The  head  of  DGHS  is  Director  General.  He  is  assisted  by  two  Additional
Directors  General.  Besides,  Directors,  Deputy  Directors,  Assistant  Directors  and  other  officers
and staffs helped him execute the responsibilities. In all medical college hospitals and divisional
levels,  directors  are  responsible  for  executive  functions.  In  medical  colleges  or  other  academic
institutions, there are principal

3.2 Capacity of Managing Procurement

Procurement in DGHS is conducted by the Project Directors and Line Directors. Most of
the  procurements  are  of  goods  and  services  in  nature;  works-related  procurements  are
done by the Public Works Department. Procurement activities are done with the existing
manpower. However, procurement consultants and other staff are also outsourced for the
procurement purpose.

Table 3.1: Capacity of Managing Procurement

Documentary Review

36

3.3 Review of Annual Procurement Plan

All APP have been studied. APPs have been prepared in line with the DPP and Operational Plan.
Findings are as under:

a. APP were not published on the website.

b. Procurement of ‘Digital Blood Glucometer + 1000 Strip with 1000 Lancet per Combo
Pac (each vial contains 25 strips x 40 Vial with 1000 lancet)’ of Package GD-19 (TID-
977460) and  GD-20 (ID-977457) of OP 3 (NCDC) had two different packages  of same
item. The packages are separated for GoB and RPA budget.

c. Procurement of MSR of OP-4 (CDC) are also with two separate packages (ID-927701
and ID-927718). The packages are separated for GoB and RPA budget.

3.4 Preparation of the Packages and Lots

As per rule 11 of PPA 2006 and rule 15 of PPR 2008, the procurement entity shall not generally
split a single procurement work into more than one package. But in order to facilitate a particular
procurement  activity  it  may  split  a  single  procurement  work  into  more  than  one  package  and
packages into more than one lot.

a. Findings

Packages and lots were prepared following the rules and explicitly mentioned in the DPP/OP and
APP and were followed during the tendering. Following are the findings:

i.  In  Project  1  (NINS)  single  package  was  made  for  GD  5  (Lot  1).  But  two  separate
contracts were made for two different items of the same lot. This is a common practice in
this project. The reasons are, same supplier cannot give all the items and the price is also
less if the items are procured from the different suppliers.

ii. In APP, procurement of Medicine (Package GD-16 of OP-3) was in DPM in a single
package to be approved by Ministry. But it was split into ten separate work orders from
two  different  suppliers  (EDCL:  6  and  BGB  Welfare  Trust:  4),  and  the  approval  power
was kept within the financial power of Line Director. The rationale for such splitting was
not mentioned in the document. The PE did not mention the description of its needs and
any special requirements concerning quality, quantity, terms and times of delivery {(Rule
75 (2)}.

iii. Procurement of ‘Digital Blood Glucometer + 1000 Strip with 1000 Lancet per Combo
Pac (each vial contains 25 strips x 40 Vial with 1000 lancet)’ of Package GD-19  (TID-
977460) and  GD-20 (ID-977457) of OP 3 (NCDC) had two different packages  but same
item. The packages are separated for GoB and RPA budget.

iv. Procurement of MSR of OP-4 (CDC) are also with two separate packages (ID-927701
and ID-927718). The packages are separated for GoB and RPA budget.

37

v.  In  OP  and  APP,  there  was  a  single  package  for  procurement  of  Unani  medicine,
Ayurvedic  medicine  and  Homeopathic  medicine  (Package  SD-6,  OP  5).  But  while
tendering  three  separate  tenders  of  a  single  package  was  done  in  e-GP  mode  and  three
separate  contracts  were  concluded  (TID-937731,  TID-959413  and  TID-959416).  The
rationale  for  such  splitting  was  not  mentioned  in  the  document.  It  was  a  mistake  done
accepted by the LD. Separate package/lot should have been catered in APP.

3.5 Advertisements in Newspaper and in Website

As per PPR 90 (2) (ka),  invitations shall be advertised in,  at least  one  Bangla language
national  newspaper  and  one  English  language  national  newspaper,  both  of  which  shall
have a wide daily circulation within Bangladesh.

a. Findings

i. Advertisement was done both in Newspapers and websites. Advertisements were given
to  both  Bangla  and  English  reputed  newspapers  like  Dainik  Bangla,  Dainik  Sangbad,
Dainik  Amader  Shomoy,  Daily  New  Age,  Dainik  Kaler  Kantho,  Manabkantha,  Dainik
Sadhinmat,  The  Daily  Star,  The  Daily  Ittefaq,  Bangladesh  Pratidin,  Daily  Observer,
Dhaka  Tribune,  The  Daily  Vorer  Kagoj,  Kalbela,  The  Daily  Amader  Orthoneeti,  The
New Nation, and Daily Industry etc.

 a.  Findings

i. OCEC was formed by the competent authority as per Rule-16(5) (ka) of PPR except for
Op-3  (NCDC).  In  80%  cases  OCEC  was  formed.  In  OP  3,  there  was  no  formal  OCEC
published by the office order; instead, the APP  price is assessed as OCEC price.

ii. TOC/POC and TEC/PEC  were formed by the competent authority as per Rule- 7 of
PPR  and  e-GP  Guidelines.  But  in  OP  3  (NCDC)  there  was  no  committee  formed  for
Financial  Year  2023-24.  Office  order  for  committee  for  Financial  Year  2022-23  was
found and referred for Financial Year 2023-24. But there was no office order/instruction.

iii. One member was included in TOC and POC from other TEC/PEC as per Rule- 7 of
PPR.

iv. External members were also included in Project 1 and OP- 5 as per Rule- 8(2) of PPR.

v. TSC was formed in all packages of project 1{(PPR Rule 8 (14)}.

3.7  Pre-Tender/Pre-Proposal Meeting

Pre-Tender meeting was conducted in project 1 and in Operational Plan 2.

3.8  Review of TER 1 to TER 4

TER 1 to TER 4 have been reviewed in detail. Those have been done as per e-GP guidelines.

3.9 Review of Contract Documents

Contract documents have been reviewed in detail. Following are the findings from the review:

a.  Additional  delivery  was  made  in  Op-2  (UHC)  and  OP  3  (NCDC).  In  OP-2,  ID-958949,
procurement  of  OT  &  Other  Equipment  for  Upazila  Health  Complexes  (10%),  ID-887110,
procurement  of  Electronic  Machinery  for  Upazila  Health  Complexes  (14.71%),  ID-885404,
procurement  of  Medicine  for  Union  Sub  Centers  (42.25%),  TID-890978,    procurement  of
Medicine for Emergency Unit & Indoor of Upazila Health Complexes (28.47%) and ID-976403,
procurement of Medicine of OT & Emergency Crisis Management for Upazila Health Complexes
(28.63%).  In  OP-3,  20%  additional  contracts  have  been  made  in  each  package:  ID-977458,
Procurement of Digital Automated Arm in BP Machine; ID-977459, Procurement of Digital BP
Machine; ID-977460, Procurement of Digital Blood Glucometer + 1000 Strips with 1000 Lancets
per  Combo  Pac  (each  vial  contains  25  strips  x  40  vials  with  1000  lancets);  and  ID-977457,
Procurement of Digital Blood Glucometer + 1000 Strips with 1000 Lancets per Combo Pac (each
vial contains 25 strips x 40 vials with 1000 lancets). The reasons for such additional orders were
the availability of the budget.

b. As per PPR rule 75 (6), there has to be a contract between two parties for DPM. There was no
formal  contract  for  DPM  signed  between  the  PE  and  the  supplier  for  procurement  of  Medicine
(Package GD-16 of OP-3). Instead, it was split into ten separate segments and ten separate work
orders  were  given  among  two  different  suppliers.    The  approval  power  was  kept  within  the
financial power of Line Director instead of Ministry.

3.10  Record Keeping

During the review of the documents and files, it was observed that generally the documents were
not  properly  maintained  and  it  took  time  to  find  out  the  documents  particularly  for  manual
procurement. Documents were also not filed properly. (Rule 43 of PPR).

i.  A total of 18 tenders have been completed using the e-GP mode, accounting for 60% of
the total tender. A total of 12 tenders were manually done, accounting for 40% of the total
tender.  7 OTM ( 6 in project 1, and 1 in OP-2), 2 QCBS, and 1 each DPM, RFQ and SSS
followed manual mode.

ii.  In  terms  of  value,  a  total  of  taka  16689.38  lakh  has  been  manually  contracted,
representing 76.03% of the total contractual value. NCT is 100% and there is no ICT.  In

the total contract value.

44

iii. Tendering  in  e-GP  mode  is  high  in  number  and percentage  but low  in  value  and its
corresponding percentage. In contrast, in manual mode, the tender number and percentage
are low but high in value, as is its corresponding percentage

iv. In Project 1, all 6 contracts followed manual mode, amounting to BDT 7815.48 lakh,
which is 35.72% of the total contract value. The PE informed that it is difficult for them
to  purchase  electromedical  equipment  in  e-GP  mode  as  all  the  equipment  are  not
possessed by a company. The equipment procured from different companies may not be
compatible with each other. If procurement is done manually and item by item, then the
cost of the equipment is less, which saves the government money, and the compatibility
of the equipment is also ensured. However, they are trying to switch over to e-GP mode.

Figure 3.4: Comparison Graph Between APP, OCEC and Contracted Price

The  above  figure  explains  a  comparison  between  total  APP  value,  OCEC  value  and
contracted price of 30 contracts. Total APP value was taka  23721.59 lakh, OCEC value
was taka 23267.04 lakh and Contracted price was taka 21951.12 lakh.

a. Findings

i.  Contracted value less than the OCE value upto 5%: 11 contracts (36.66%).

ii. Contracted value less than the OCE value of 5.01% to 10%: 3 contracts (10%).

iii. Contracted value less than the OCE value of 10.01% to 15%: 4 contracts (13.33%).

iv. Contracted value less than the OCE value above 15%: 10 contracts (33.33%)

v. The contracted value is equal to the OCE value : 1 contract (3.33).

vi. Contract value could not be ascertained with the OCE value: 1 contract (3.33%)

vii. Overall contracted price 8.13% less than the OCE price.

viii. OCE price was same that of APP price in OP 2 and OP 3.

ix.  OCE price needs to be more realistic.

47

Competition in Tender

Rule 13(1) of the PPA 2006 and rule 18 of the PPR 2008 describes that, the PE shall ensure that
competition in procurement takes place on the basis of impartial and objective terms by providing
all  necessary  information  to  all  prospective  applicants,  tenderers  or  consultants  required  for  the
preparation of the application, tender, quotation or proposals. Rule also says to provide reasonable
access  for  visits  by  potential Tenderers  to sites  specified  for  work  or  project  which  is  vague  in
nature  as  ‘reasonable  time’  can  be  interpreted  in  different  manner  from  time  to  time.  In  this
regard, a complete profile of  state of competition in procurement in DGHS has been explained in
subsequent tables.

a. Findings

i. Total number of tender documents sold is 187, and average number of tender
documents sold per package or lot is 6.23.

ii. Total number of tenders submitted is 138, and average number of tenders submitted per
package or lot is 4.6.

iii. Total number of responsive tenders is 84, and average number of responsive tenders
per package or lot is 2.8.

iv. Number of tenders with at least five and more responsive tenderers: 7 (23.33%).

v. Number of tenders with at least three to four responsive tenderers: 4 (13.33%).

vi. Number of tenders with at least two responsive tenderers: 6 (20%).

vii.  Number  of  single  responsive  and  single  tenderers:  13  (43.33%).  The  number  and
percentage of single tenders are high and less competition.

viii. Other than Op-5, single responsive tenders are most common all projects/OP.

ix. Single tenders’ submissions are mostly in project 1. Reasons in this regard have been
given  by  the  PD,  which  has  been  attached  to  this  report.  Single  tender  submission  and

49

single tenders are due to the specialty of the electromedical equipment, vaccine, and the
procurement  method  by  the  single  source  selection  method.  Research  on  Survey  to
Estimate the Prevalence of Biomarkers of Infection with Hepatitis B Virus and Hepatitis
C Virus among Children 2-5 years was done as an intellectual and professional services
from ICDDR,B by single source selection method.

Figure 3.5:  Competition in Tender

According  to  the  above  figure,  five  and  more  responsive  tenderers  were  23.33%,  three  to  four
responsive tenderers were 13.33%, two responsive tenderers were 20%, and single responsive and
single tenderers were 43.33%.

11, 13, 14, 15, 16,17, 29 and 30.

vi. The contract completion time of package GD-16, procurement of medicine by DPM,
could not be ascertained as there was no contract completion date and actual delivery date
of  goods  found  in  the  LD  office.  However,  delivery  of  medicine  by  EDCL  and  BGB
Welfare Trust on 12 January 25 and 8 August 24, respectively, has been ascertained from
the field visits.

ii.    In  OP  2,  delay  also  occurred  in  case  of    ID-976403,  where  supplier  mentioned  that
delay  occurred  due  to  the  global  scenario  and  the  dollar  crisis,  Eid  leave,  labour  crisis,
additional  order,    there  is  a  problem  with  imports  due  to  the  delay  in  letter  of  credit
placement. But during review it has been found that inspection was completed on 19 June
2024 (before the actual supply date).

supplier  and  receipt  from  all    recipients  were  taken  and  submitted  to  NCDC  by  the
supplier. Same is the case with ID-977460 and ID-977457, Procurement of digital blood
glucometer + 1000 Strip with 1000 Lancet per Combo Pac (each vial contains 25 strips x
40 Vial with 1000 lancet).

vi. In OP 2 (UHC) and OP 3 (NCDC), the delivery and distribution of medicine and MSR
to the hospitals/ Upazilas are done by the supplier which took time, and time was overrun.
In  OP  4  (CDC),  medicine  and  MSRs  were  distributed  to  the  stores  at  Dhaka  by  the
supplier and the collection from Dhaka is done by the hospitals and Upazilas. There was
no delay in OP 4 (CDC).

storage without any action, and thereby their condition is deteriorating, causing a loss of
government money.

Findings:

Audit Objection Status

3.31 Audit objections are as under:

a. Project 1 (NINS). Audit was not held during the period of review.

b. Operational Plan-2 (UHC)

i. In the office of the Line Director, Upazila Health Care (UHC), Mohakhali. Dhaka of 4
HPNSP under Health Service Division at the financial year 2023-2024, there is financial
loss of Govt amounting to Tk. 90,30,000.00 due to purchase of medical equipment from
preferred highest bidder by rendering responsive lowest bidder as non-responsive.

ii. While auditing the office of the Line Director. UHC, bill/vouchers of Procurement of
OT & other Equipment and related tender documents (e-GP Tender ID no. 958949) were
examined.  On  details  scrutiny  the  audited  documents  shows  that  the  local  authority
purchased  03  (three)  kinds  of  OT  &  other  Equipment  from  the  supplier  Bangladesh
Science House  whose quoted rate was Tk. 3,15,00,000.00, where the  quoted rate of the
lowest bidder Bogra Trade Centre was Tk 2,24,70,000.00 and made him non responsive
because  of  according  to  evaluation  report-1  tenderer  information  is  not  complete.
According  to  evaluation  report-2  eligibility  of  Goods,  technical  specification,  tenderers
qualification is not acceptable, the Line of Credit (LOC) not as per STD and among the
03  (three)  equipment  OT  Light  ceiling,  Diathermy  Machine  acceptable  but  the  offered
model  of  Ultrasonogram  Machine  with  printer  is  not  found.  Here  it  is  found  that  the
lowest bidder Bogra Trade Centre comply all the Terms and Conditions of the Tender On
the basis of this cause Govt. has financially  loosed (3,15,00,000.00-2,24,70,000.00)=Tk.
90,30,000.00

iii.  As  per  section  31(1)  (e)  of  PPA,  2006  the  contract  shall  be  awarded  to  the  lowest
evaluated responsive bidder. According to the rule 98(7) (a)  of PPR 2008,  a TEC  may
regard a Tender as responsive even if it contains minor or insignificant deviations, which
do not meaningfully alter or depart from the technical specifications. characteristics and
commercial terms and conditions or other requirements set out in the Tender Document
and any such deviations shall be quantified in monetary terms, as far as possible: But the

62

Procuring Entity did not follow the above mentioned rule. That causes a financial loss of
Govt.

c. Operational Plan-3 (NCDC)

i.  In  the  office  of  the  Line  Director,  Non-Communicable  Disease  Control  (NCDC),
Mohakhali. Dhaka of 4 HPNSP under Health Service Division at the financial year 2023-
2024,  there  is  financial  loss  of  Govt  amounting  to  Tk.  4,08,41,650.00  due  to  without
receiving medical machinery. During the audit, tender documents, contract, work orders,
and  bill  vouchers  were  examined.  While  auditing  it  was  found  that  ID-977458,
Procurement of Digital Automated Arm in BP Machine from Biosys Technologies, out of
120  pcs  Digital  Automated  Arm  in  BP  Machine  only  quantity  14  pcs  were  supplied.
During  the  procurement  of  Digital  BP  Machine  and  Digital  blood  glucometer  +  1000
Strip with 1000 Lancet per Combo Pac (each vial contains 25 strips x 40 Vial with 1000
lancet)  from  Azmeer  International,  payment  was  made  before  the  full  delivery  of  the
contracted items.

ii.  In  the  office  of  the  Line  Director,  Non-Communicable  Disease  Control  (NCDC),
Mohakhali. Dhaka of 4 HPNSP under Health Service Division at the financial year 2023-
2024, there is financial loss of Govt amounting to Tk. 19,87,03,350.00 due to payment to
the  supplier  without  receiving  goods  until  audit  date.  During  the  audit,  bill  voucher,
MOU, stocks were examined. While auditing it was found that Essential Drugs Company
Limited did not supply the medicines but the payment was made by the procuring entity.

d. Operational Plan-4 (CDC). Audit was not held during the period of review.

e. Operational Plan-5 (AMC)

i. In the office of the Line Director, Alternative Medical Care (AMC), Mohakhali. Dhaka
of  4  HPNSP  under  Health  Service  Division  at  the  financial  year  2023-2024,  there  is
financial  loss  of  Govt  amounting  to  Tk.  48,50000.00  due  to  non-delivery    medical
machinery. During the audit, tender documents, contract, work orders, and bill vouchers
were  examined.  While  auditing  it  was  found  that  ID-958441  Procurement  of  Laboratory
Instruments  from  M/S  Prantik  Enterprise,  the  bill  was  paid  to  the  supplier  but  the
instruments  were  not  accepted  by  the  Unani  and  Ayurvedic  Medical  College  and
Hospital.

Progress on Recommendations on Financial Year 2022-23 Procurement Post Review

3.32  Recommendations of Financial Year 2022-23 Procurement Post Review

Following were some observations of the last procurement post review:

a. Procurement packages of goods and services should be categorized in such a manner
that ensure competitiveness of the tendering process within the tender validity period.

63

b.  Committee  approval  for  an  official  cost  estimate  should  be  taken  and  it  should  be
package/ contract specific.

c.  Office  order  must  be  kept  regarding  constitution  of  the  TOC  and  TEC  in  every
procurement file and contract management issues should be carefully dealt with.

d.  Minimum  days  allowed  for  preparation  and  submission  of  tender  should  be  strictly
followed by the PEs.

e. In some Operational Plans and Projects, the competition in procurement process in the
form  of  Tender  Participation  is  unexpectedly  weak.  Thus,  all  efforts  to  enhance  the
competition should be taken with high priority.

f. For the extension of the Tender Validity Period, PE should comply with the conditions
of the extension of the Tender Validity Period.

g.  The  prescribed  formats  may  be  directly  adopted  or  be  modified  according  to
organizational  needs.  The  monitoring  log  should  be  attached  to  the  procurement  file  so
that  the  concerned  officers  may  have  the  knowledge  of  both the  expired  and  remaining
time at any given period.

h.  PE  needs  to  be  more  attentive  towards  procurement  processing  lag/lead-times.  All
procurement processing activities should be done within the time allocated in the PPR and
e-GP guidelines.

i. PE should keep a time log as a tool to monitor the Evaluation and Approval status of the
procurement to timely award the contract.

j.  Performance  security/performance  guarantee’s  validity  period  shall  be  extended
accordingly if contract time is extended.

k. Need to mention the contract number in all contract agreements to identify the contract
and maintain a register of contracts.

l. Officials and relevant staffs should be trained on procurement related topics and 100%
procurements should be done by e-GP.

3.33    Progress  on  the  Recommendations  of  Financial  Year  2022-23  Procurement
Post Review

Actions are being taken to rectify the problems. However, there are still some areas like increase
competition, procurement processing lag/lead-times, different time compliances, following e-GP,
and enhancing the capacity including imparting training needs to be given more emphasis.

64

Summary of the Evaluation

After  detailed  evaluation,  a  summary  is  given  in  the  table  below  that  shows  the  different
indicators of the DGHS regarding their procurement performance in the financial year 2023-24.

67

Summary of Findings

After evaluating thirty selected contracts, other contract-related documentation, discussions with
procurement  personnel,  and  field  visits  and  inspections,  the  following  is  a  summary  of  the
findings, which are given in succeeding paragraphs:

a. Procurement Management Capacity.   Procurement at DGHS is handled by the Project and
Line  Directors.  Most  of  the  procurements  are  of  goods  and  services  in  nature;  works-related
procurements are done by the Public Works Department. Procurement activities are done with the
existing  manpower.  Procurement  personnel  are  inadequate  and  are  also  outsourced.  Training  of
the personnel on PPR and e-GP is also inadequate.

b.  Preparation  of  the  Annual  Procurement  Plan.  APPs  have  been  prepared  in  line  with  the
DPP and Operational Plan. However, APP are not published in the website.

c. Preparation of the Packages and Lots. Packages and lots were prepared following the rules
and explicitly mentioned in the DPP/OP and APP and were followed during the tendering except
few  cases.  In  project  1  where    single  package  was  made  for  GD  5  (Lot  1),  but  two  separate
contracts  were  made  for two  different items  of  the  same  lot.  In  APP,  procurement  of  Medicine
(Package GD-16 of OP-3) was in DPM in a single package to be approved by Ministry, but it was
split into ten separate work orders from two different suppliers. In OP and APP, there was a single
package  for  procurement  of  Unani  medicine,  Ayurvedic  medicine  and  Homeopathic  medicine
(Package SD-6, OP 5). But while tendering three separate tenders of a single package was done in
e-GP mode and three separate contracts were concluded.

d.  Advertisements  in  Newspaper  and  in  Website.    Advertisement  was  done  both  in
Newspapers.

e. Formation of Different Committees.  OCEC was formed by the competent authority except
for Op-3. TOC/POC and TEC/PEC  were formed by the competent authority as per PPR and e-GP
Guidelines. One member was included in TOC and POC from other TEC/PEC. External members
were also included in Project 1 and OP- 5. TSC was formed in all packages of project 1.

f.    Pre-Tender/Pre-Proposal  Meeting.  Pre-Tender  meeting  was  conducted  in  project  1  and  in
Operational Plan 2.

g.  Review  of  Contract  Documents.  Additional  Contracts  ranging  from  10%  to  42.25%  were
made  in  Op-2  and  OP  3.  The  reasons  for  such  additional  orders  were  the  availability  of  the
budget.  There  was  no  formal  contract  for  DPM  signed  between  the  PE  and  the  supplier  for
procurement of Medicine (Package GD-16 of OP-3).

68

h. Record Keeping. Generally, the documents were not properly maintained and it took time to
find  out  the  documents  particularly  for  manual  procurement.  Documents  were  also  not  filed
properly.

i.  Preparation  of  Annual  Procurement  Plan.  Out  of  131  packages/lots  in  the  APP,  119  were
invited  for tendering,  accounting  for  90.83% of all tenders  invited  against the  APP.  Out of 119
tenders, 101 contracts were signed, accounting for 84.87% of contracts signed against APP. The
overall planning was good.

j. Procurement Category, Types and Methods. Out of 30 selected contracts, 26 are in the goods
category, accounting for 86.66% of the total tender; and 4 are in the services category, accounting
for  13.34%  of  the  total  tender.  83.33%  of  the  tender  under  review  followed  OTM,  with  6.67%
using QCBS and 3.33% each using DPM, RFQ, and SSS methods. 100% of the tenders followed
National Competitive Tendering.

k.  Tenders followed  Manual  and  e-GP  Mode.    60%  of the  selected  tenders used  e-GP  mode,
while  40% were done manually, but in contrast their contracted value was  23.97% and 76.03%
respectively.

l.  Budget  and  Procurement  Rules  Followed.  A  total  of  7  out  of  30  tenders  were  funded  by
development partners which is 23.33% of the total number of tenders. All contracts followed the
PPR 2008 and e-GP guidelines.

m. Financial Planning and Management. The total contracted price was 8.13% lower than the
OCE price. OEC price was same that of APP price in OP 2 and OP 3. However, OCE price needs
to be more realistic.

n.  Competition  in  Tender.  The  number  and  percentage  of  single  tender  and  single  responsive
tenders  among  the  selected  contracts  are  high  with  43.33%  of  total  contracts  and  82.88%  total
contracted value shared by 5 tenderers. Single tender submission and single tenders are due to the
specialty of the electromedical equipment, vaccine, and the single-source selection method.

o. Contract Implementation Time. Out of 30 contracts, 18 contracts were implemented within
time  limit  with  average  time  overrun  of  433  days.  No  LD  was  imposed  for  the  delay.  Delays
occurred for tendering and contracting at the fag end of the financial  year in the month of June
and also for contracting additional delivery, and also for late delivery by the supplier.

p.  Time  Compliance.  Adequate  time  was  given  for  the  preparation  of  all  tenders.  Out  of  30
tenders 25 tenders were evaluated within the time line. 22 tenders were approved within the time
line by the competent authority. All  NoA were issued timely, and 24  tenders followed the DoFP.

p.  Field  Visit/Inspection.  Some  goods  like  Raw  materials  and  Laboratory  instruments  were
procured without the requirement. Equipment has been delivered but not installed and kept in the
storage  for  long  time.    The  condition  of  some  equipment  has  deteriorated  in  the  storehouse.
Storage condition also needs improvement.

g. Complaints and Appeal. There was no case of complaints and appeal.

69

h. Payment Management. Payment was made prior to the delivery of goods and services in 9 out
of 30 contracts. In case of Laboratory Instruments, goods were rejected by the inspection team but
payment was made before inspection and acceptance.

SWOT Analysis

Strengths

i. Detailed tender/proposal evaluation.
ii.  No complaints and appeal case.
iii. Use of professional advisors, such as Consultants, to augment procurement capability.

Weaknesses

i.   Limited supplier options for certain categories of supplier.
ii.  Less Competition.
iii. Time overruns.
iv.  Lacking in needs assessment.
v.   Improper maintenance of the equipment.
vi.  Resource constraints particularly trained manpower
vii.  Contract and additional contract at the fag end of the financial year.
viii. Weak contract management.

Opportunities

i.  Study and conduct market research for emerging markets for potential suppliers.
ii. Training and development of the staff.
iii. Review of the procurement capacity.

Threats

i. Supplier’s irrational justifications for not maintaining the delivery schedule.
ii. Dependency on the Suppliers for delivery.
iii. Reputational risk for late delivery of the medicine.

70

Chapter Four

Recommendations

While  the  leadership  of  DGHS  strives  for  transparency  and  accountability  in  procurement  to
ensure the best use of government funds, there is always room for improvement and refinement.
Based  on the  evaluation  and  findings, the following recommendations are  made  to improve  the
effectiveness and efficiency of the procurement process:

4.1 Capacity Enhancement and Imparting Training. The need for training on procurement is
very  much  felt  in  DGHS.  Steps  should  be  taken  to  train  the  staff  at  all  levels  involved  in  the
procurement  process.  Steps  may  also  be  taken  to  train  the  trainers  and  then  impart  in-house
training. (Findings 3.2.a. P.24).

4.2 Justification for Splitting of the Package. Splitting of packages from DPP/OP/APP should
preferably  be  avoided.  However,  if  it  is  unavoidable,  it  should  be  done  with  appropriate
justification.  The  issues  of  duplication  of  efforts  during  the  procurement  stage,  post-contract
management  stage,  and  documentation  should  be  kept  in  mind  while  splitting  the  packages.
(Findings 3.4.a. P.26).

4.3  Efficient  Management  and  Execution  of  DPM.  The  DPM  should  be  justified  for
procurement needs and delivery time, and implemented within the time limit. DPM approved in
the APP should not be split into small packages with the obligations of seeking the approval of
higher  authority.  The  goods  procured  through    DPM  should  also  be  taken  from  appropriate
supplier,  and  trading  through  DPM  should  be  avoided.  (Findings  3.4.  a.  ii.  P.  25,  3.9.  b.  P.28,
3.21. P. 42 and 3.26. a. i. P.47).

4.4  Increased  Competition  in  Tenders.  Efforts  should  be  made  for  more  competitive
tendering.  Efforts should  be  made  to  explore  opportunities  to  diversify  the  supplier  base  within
categories with limited options. (Findings 3.17. P.36, 3.18.a. 3.19, & 3.20.a. P 37-43).

4.5  Evaluating  the  Correct  Needs.  A  needs  analysis  is  a  crucial  in  the  procurement,
identifying and prioritizing organizational  requirements. The needs assessment of DGHS should
be carried out accurately to ensure that the purchased goods are used effectively for their intended
purpose. It will ensure that government money is spent efficiently, effectively, and economically.
(Findings 3.25.a. iii, P. 47 and 3.28.a. i, ii P.48).

71

4.6 Steps for Achieving Value for Money. Efforts should be made to maximize the value of
each  purchase  or  sum  of  money  spent.  Value  for  money  is  determined  not  only  by  the  lowest
purchase price, but also by the maximum economy, efficiency, and effectiveness of the purchase.
It  is  also  recommended  that  DGHS  may  resort  to  systematic  analytical  process,  which  would
examine the effectiveness of a projects and operational plan for evaluating its actual results. This
can include impact evaluation (the difference it made in the health sector), economic evaluation
(whether its benefits justified its costs) and process evaluation ( the process of its delivery to the
Upazila). It should be considered as part of the decision-making process from the preparation of
the  DPP/OP,  APP,  tendering  and  contract  process  and  particularly  during  post  contract
management. Since all the OP have been completed in financial year 2023-24, therefore, DGHS
may assess value for money achieved of the OP. The following practices should be adhered to for
achieving the value for money:

a. Procured goods should be delivered within the specific delivery time. Non delivery of
medicine for prolonged time should be avoided. (Findings 3.21.a. P.43).

b.  Procured  goods  should  not  be  left  idle  or  unoperated  for  a  prolonged  period,  as  this
could lead to equipment degradation and decreased value for money. (Findings 3.25.a. iii
P. 47).

c. The procured goods should have enough expired life. (Findings 3.26.a. i P. 47).

d. When a goods/equipment is procured, its related accessories’ needs also to be procured
for  its  smooth  functioning  in  future  so  that  optimum  utilization  of  the  procured
goods/equipment is done for achieving value for money. (Findings 3.26.a. iii P. 47).

e. Medicines, raw materials and medical equipment should be kept in appropriate storage
condition and inventory should be properly maintained. (Findings 3.25.a. iii P. 47, 3.27. a.
ii . P. 48 and 3.28. a. iii. P. 48).

4.7 Adhering to Different Compliance Issues.  Adhering  to  the  different rules  of  the  PPR
2008 is essential for maintaining transparency and accountability in the procurement. PE should
follow the different compliance issues mentioned in the PPR 2008 to avoid potential procurement
risks. As a result, it is critical to ensure their compliance through the following practices:

a. APP should be published on the website. (Findings 3.23 P. 25).

b. Documents should be maintained properly. (Findings 3.10.P. 28).

c. Efforts should be taken for 100% procurement following e-GP. (Findings 3.14. P.32).

d.  OCEC  should  be  formed  in  all  OP  and  the  OCEC  price  should  be  more  realistic.
(Findings 3.6.a. P. 27 & 3.16.a.viii. P. 34-35).

e. Time extension should be approved by competent authority. (Findings 3.21.a.v. P. 43).

f.  The  timetable  prescribed  in  the  PPR  2008  should  be  maintained  for  the  tender
evaluation, and approval from the competent authority. (Findings 3.23 P. 44-45).

g. LD should be imposed where appropriate for delay. (Findings 3.21.P. 42-43).

72

h. DoFP should be followed. (Findings 3.23.a.v. P.45).

4.8 Need for Efficient Budget and Financial Management

Estimating  budget  with  procurement  timeframe  and  aligning  the  budget  within  the
timeline is important for an efficient financial and budget management. Budget planning
and timeline of DGHS should be based on realistic assumptions so that tender, contracts
and delivery of goods are well planned within the financial year. The following practices
should be adhered to for an efficient budget and financial management:

a. Tender should be held, and a contract, including additional delivery, should be made in
such a manner so that delivery of the contracted goods can be made within the financial
year, and the payment can be made after the delivery within the financial year. (Findings
3.9.a. P 28 and 3.30. P. 49).

b.  Practice of  payment  by  the  PE before the  delivery  of  goods  should  be avoided  at  all
cost.                   (Findings 3.30. P. 49).

c.  Payment  should  not  be  made  before  the  acceptance  of  the  goods  by  the  end  users.
(Findings 3.28 a. ii. P. 48 and 3.30. Serial 29. P. 49).

4.9  Timely  Implementation  of  the  Contract.  Efforts  should  be  made  for  the  timely
execution of the contract. For this, a post-contract management strategy is essential. There may be
a  monitoring  team  from  PE  that  should  monitor  the  progress  of  the  contract.  (Findings  3.21.a.
P.45).

Conclusion

The  BPPA  encourages  effective  resource  use,  transparency,  accountability,  efficiency,  and  fair
competition  in  the  public  procurement.  It  also  uses  resources  and  independent  consultants  to
review  procurement  records,  including  one  that  reviews  the  post-procurement  contracts  for  the
DGHS for the financial year 2023–2024. The selection of projects/operational plans and contracts
for  review  has  been  meticulous,  considering  various  procurement  types,  methods,  categories,
contract  values.  Projects/operational  plans  were  funded  by  the  GoB  and  development  partners,
and all the contracts followed PPR and e-GP  guidelines. DGHS signed all contracts against the
invited  tenders.  However, the  number  of single  tender  and  single  responsive tenders  among  the
selected contracts are high. Time compliances were generally good but contract implementation
took more than the time given in contract. The proper needs assessment and obtaining value for
the  money  of  the  purchased  goods  and  services  should  be  the  main  priorities  of  DGHS.    They
must also be able to manage their time effectively in order for contracts to be fulfilled within the
financial  year  and  for  payments  to  be  paid  following  the  delivery  of  goods  and  services.  Staff
training is still a crucial component of improving procurement capability of DGHS. While there
may be some unintended errors, the ultimate objective of the review is to help improve the DGHS
procurement system.

73
